Empty dropzone --></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/medium/am0c21676_0009.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0009.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c21676&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Bimetallic oxides have received considerable attention as anodes for lithium/sodium-ion batteries (LIBs/SIBs) due to their high electrochemical activity and theoretical specific capacity. However, their cycling performance is limited by large volume variation, severe aggregation, and pulverization of bimetallic oxide nanoparticles during repeated metal ion insertion/extraction processes. Herein, bimetallic antimony–vanadium oxide nanoparticles embedded in graphene (SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G) composites are prepared by a one-step hydrothermal method. Bimetallic SbVO<sub>4</sub> with abundant redox reaction sites can provide high specific capacity by a multi-electron reaction. A robust graphene substrate can not only alleviate volume expansion but also prevent aggregation and collapse of highly active bimetallic SbVO<sub>4</sub>. Due to the excellent synergy between the two building components, SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G hybrids exhibit excellent electrochemical activity, structural stability, and electrochemical performance. When employed as anodes for LIBs and SIBs, SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites display excellent cycling performance (1079.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles for LIBs and 401.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 450 cycles for SIBs) and impressive rate capability. This work demonstrates that SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites are promising anodes for both LIBs and SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D21127%26pageCount%3D11%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DYutong%2BHao%252C%2BYing%2BJiang%252C%2BLuzi%2BZhao%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D18%26contentID%3Dacsami.0c21676%26title%3DBimetallic%2BAntimony%25E2%2580%2593Vanadium%2BOxide%2BNanoparticles%2BEmbedded%2Bin%2BGraphene%2Bfor%2BStable%2BLithium%2Band%2BSodium%2BStorage%26numPages%3D11%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1944-8244%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Daamick%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D21137%26publicationDate%3DMay%2B2021" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2021 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><div class="article__tags article__tags--subjects"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Subjects</h3><div tabindex="0" class="article__tags__tooltip"><div aria-describedby="tooltip-subjects" class="article__tags__icon"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are subjects</span></div><p id="tooltip-subjects" role="tooltip" class="article__tags__diff">Article subjects are automatically applied from the ACS Subject Taxonomy and describe the scientific concepts and themes of the article.</p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291445&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Chemical structure" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Chemical%20structure" class="article__tags__link">Chemical structure</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291331&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Composites" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Composites" class="article__tags__link">Composites</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=291371&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Electrodes" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Electrodes" class="article__tags__link">Electrodes</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=290920&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Nanoparticles" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Nanoparticles" class="article__tags__link">Nanoparticles</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"><a href="/action/doSearch?ConceptID=292522&amp;ref=fulltext" title="Two dimensional materials" data-id="article_abstract_Subject_Two%20dimensional%20materials" class="article__tags__link">Two dimensional materials</a></li></ul></div></div></div></div><div class="article__tags article__tags--keywords hlFld-Abstract"><div class="article__tags__container"><header class="article__tags__header"><h3 class="article__tags__heading">Keywords</h3><div class="article__tags__tooltip" tabindex="0"><div class="article__tags__icon" aria-describedby="tooltip-keywords"><i class="icon-info-circle-outline"></i><span class="sr-only">what are keywords</span></div><p class="article__tags__diff" id="tooltip-keywords" role="tooltip">
                        Article keywords are supplied by the authors and highlight key terms and topics
                        of the paper.
                    </p></div></header><ul class="article__tags__list"><li class="article__tags__item"><a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Graphene&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_graphene">graphene</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sbvo4&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_SbVO4">SbVO<sub>4</sub></a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Synergistic+Effect&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_synergistic%20effect">synergistic effect</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Lithium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_lithium-ion%20batteries">lithium-ion batteries</a></li><li class="article__tags__item"> <a class="article__tags__link" href="https://pubs.acs.org/action/doSearch?action=search&amp;AllField=Sodium-ion+Batteries&amp;qsSearchArea=AllField&amp;ref=fulltext" data-id="article_abstract_Keyword_sodium-ion%20batteries">sodium-ion batteries</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody"><div id="sec1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i2">Introduction</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">In recent years, it is urgent to develop low-cost and high-capacity energy storage systems with the continuous development of the industrial society and national economy. Lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) have been widely used in computers, mobile phones, sports bracelets, and other portable electronic devices. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref1 ref2 ref3'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> However, current LIBs cannot fulfill the demand of grid-scale energy storage because of the limitation of lithium resource, cost, and security issues. Therefore, sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered a strong competitor for large-scale energy storage devices because sodium is rich in resources and they have a similar working mechanism to LIBs. Although LIB materials with good performance are also applied to SIBs, the performance of SIBs is unsatisfactory due to the large ion radius of sodium. For example, graphite exhibited low capacity and poor stability when used as anodes for SIBs, whereas it has achieved commercial development in the anode of LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4" aria-label="reference 4">(4)</a> However, whether in LIBs or SIBs, the development of anodes with high electrochemical performance is still required.</div><div class="NLM_p">Tremendous efforts have been made in the exploration of suitable anodes with superior properties for LIBs and SIBs, such as carbon-based materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5" aria-label="reference 5">(5)</a> oxides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref6" aria-label="reference 6">(6)</a> sulfides, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref7" aria-label="reference 7">(7)</a> alloy materials, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref8'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref8" aria-label="reference 8">(8)</a> and titanium-based materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10" aria-label="reference 9 and 10">(9,10)</a> Among them, vanadium-based oxides are regarded as one of the most promising anodes for LIBs and SIBs. There are various reserves of vanadium-based oxides, such as VO<sub>2</sub>, V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub>, and V<sub>3</sub>O<sub>7</sub>·H<sub>2</sub>O. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a> In addition, vanadium-based oxide materials with wide channel structures only provide sufficient space to accommodate Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ions and also contribute to high specific capacities by multi-electron conversion reactions. Vanadium-based oxides have been widely studied in lithium/sodium storage. For example, VO<sub>2</sub> with a stable open framework provides rapid Li<sup>+</sup> ion intercalation/deintercalation. The hollow spherical structure of radial VO<sub>2</sub> nanowires alleviated the volume change by self-shrinking and self-expanding, exhibiting 80% of the initial capacity over 1000 cycles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref12" aria-label="reference 12">(12)</a> In addition, the inherent dominant pseudocapacitance behavior with enhanced ion diffusion kinetics is conducive to good rate performance. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> Two-dimensional thin VO<sub>2</sub> (B) nanosheets with low intercalation energy barriers as well as a short diffusion length achieved good cycling performance and an enhanced rate capacity of 140 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 100C for LIB anodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a> However, single metal oxides have encountered low electronic conductivity, which leads to unsatisfactory discharge capacity and rate performance. The bimetallic oxide could possess higher electrochemical activity relative to the single metal oxide anode, which is able to improve the conductivity and increase the specific capacity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref15" aria-label="reference 15">(15)</a> Therefore, binary metal oxides have drawn increasing attention in recent years. For instance, the theoretical capacity of Na-containing vanadium oxide (NaVO<sub>3</sub>) is 220 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> It can maintain a stable capacity in LIB anodes during the discharge/charge process, but the capacity declines quickly in SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a> In another study, Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical porous microparticles were synthesized by the surface solvation treatment of Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> with ethanol. The obtained Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> hierarchical porous microparticles displayed high initial discharge capacities of 799 and 382 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> in LIBs and SIBs, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18" aria-label="reference 18">(18)</a> Recently, a P-containing Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub>/N-doped carbon-coated nanowire structure was synthesized and studied for lithium storage. A high capacity of 1002 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.5 A g<sup>–1</sup> was obtained due to the considerable specific surface area and increased conductivity, as well as the robust structure provided by the P “bridge” between Fe<sub>2</sub>VO<sub>4</sub> and carbon sheets. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref19" aria-label="reference 19">(19)</a> Despite great progress achieved in bimetallic oxides, it still remains a significant challenge to meet the overall electrochemical performance for both LIBs and SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p">Bimetallic antimony–vanadium oxides (SbVO<sub>4</sub>) possess superior electrochemical activity by virtue of their dense crystal structure, composition, interface, and active sites. For SbVO<sub>4</sub>, different reactions take place in SIBs and LIBs. In LIBs, along with the insertion of Li<sup>+</sup> ions, SbVO<sub>4</sub> is reduced to metallic Sb and V, where V improves the electronic conductivity. The alloying reaction of Li and Sb takes place in the process of further lithiation. In SIBs, only conversion and alloying of Sb take place in sodiation. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> The multistep reaction of Sb can significantly increase the capacity in theory. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> When the SbVO<sub>4</sub> materials are used as anodes for LIBs, they achieved a high initial capacity of 650 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 2.5–0.5 V. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> However, the self-agglomeration and volume expansion of some SbVO<sub>4</sub> particles resulted in the structural inability of Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> ions to insert and extract, which further leads to the loss of capacity. In this regard, constructing a composite structure with SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles and a conductive substrate is anticipated to be an effective method to enhance the cycling property for both LIBs and SIBs.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Herein, we successfully prepared antimony–vanadium oxide nanoparticles embedded in graphene (SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G) by a one-step hydrothermal method. Coupled with the subsequent freeze-drying process, the isolation of the SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles is uniformly dispersed on the graphene substrate. Bimetallic SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G with abundant redox reaction sites can offer extraordinary electrochemical properties, and its nanoscale size shortens the ion/electron transport distance and improves the reaction kinetics for lithium and sodium storage. Graphene serving as the underlying support not only effectively alleviates volume expansion but also prevents the SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles from aggregation during repeated Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction processes. The SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles, in turn, act as the spacer to prevent graphene nanosheets from restacking, thus maintaining the active areas to the most extent. The synergistic effect between the components of SbVO<sub>4</sub> and graphene with excellent structural integrity and electrochemical activity ensures an outstanding lithium/sodium storage performance. As anticipated, the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites display impressive cycling stability (1079.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles for LIBs anode and 401.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 450 cycles for SIBs anode) and good rate performance as anodes for LIBs and SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i3">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i4" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i4"> Material Preparation</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Graphene oxides were prepared via a modified Hummers method. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> Typically, the suspension was obtained by dispersing the graphene oxide powder into deionized water with advanced ultrasonication. SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites were prepared by hydrothermal and subsequent freeze-drying. First, 0.234 g of the NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> powder was dissolved into 50 mL of deionized water with a water bath at 60 °C. Second, 0.456 g of the SbCl<sub>3</sub> powder was added to that solution. Subsequently, 10 mL of the graphene suspension (2.5 mg mL<sup>–1</sup>) was dropped into the solution and stirred for 15 min. The mixed liquid was transferred to a 100 mL Teflon-lined autoclave and heated at 180 °C for 24 h. Finally, SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites were obtained by liquid nitrogen freeze-drying. As a comparison sample, a pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> material was synthesized by the same process without the addition of the graphene suspension.</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i5" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i5"> Material Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The structure of the materials was characterized by X-ray diffraction (XRD, DMAX-2400, Cu Kα with a wavelength of 1.5406 Å, Japan). Raman spectroscopy was performed on an RFS100/S with a wavelength of 532 nm. The elemental composition and chemical valence were determined using an X-ray photoelectron spectrometer (XPS, PHI-1600 ESCA, U.K.). The morphologies were determined by scanning electron microscopy (SEM, Quanta 600, Netherlands) with an energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) detector.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Electrochemical Measurements</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The working electrode was mixed with the active material Super P and the binder carboxymethylcellulose sodium (CMC-Na) according to a mass ratio of 7:2:1. The mass loading of the active substance was about 1–1.2 mg cm<sup>–2</sup>. The lithium metal served as the counter/reference electrode, 1 M LiPF<sub>6</sub> in EC/DMC (1:1) was used as the electrolyte, and Celgard 2400 was used as a separator. LIBs were assembled using CR2025 coin cells. In addition, the electrochemical measurements of SIBs were performed on CR2032 coin cells. Na metal, 1 M NaClO<sub>4</sub> in EC/DMC (1:1), and Whatman glass fiber were used as a reference electrode, electrolyte, and separator, respectively. Galvanostatic charge/discharge (GCD) tests were performed on a LAND CT2001A at 0.01–3.0 V. The scanning voltage ranges of cyclic voltammetry (CV) were tested on a CHI604E electrochemical workstation with the voltage ranging from 0.01 to 3.0 V. Electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) was performed at 10<sup>5</sup> to 0.01 Hz. The EIS experiments for LIBs and SIBs were performed at the open-circuit voltage, and the bias potential was set as 0.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i7">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p">The preparation of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites is described in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">1</a> and the details are given in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec2" aria-label="Experimental Section">Experimental Section</a>. First, SbCl<sub>3</sub> was put into an NH<sub>4</sub>VO<sub>3</sub> solution to form a Sb–V-containing solution. Second, a dispersed graphene suspension was added dropwise to the aforementioned solution. Subsequently, the mixed liquid was transferred to a hydrothermal kettle for the hydrothermal treatment. Finally, the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites were obtained after freeze-drying. The XRD patterns of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites and SbVO<sub>4</sub> are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>a. Compared with pure SbVO<sub>4</sub>, the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites indicated no other phase except for SbVO<sub>4</sub>, where peaks are indexed to tetragonal SbVO<sub>4</sub> with a space group of <i>P</i>42/<i>mnm</i> (PDF #77-0331) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>b). The peaks of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites become broader, which may result from the encapsulated nanostructure by graphene. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24" aria-label="reference 24">(24)</a> Raman spectra were further recorded to reveal the structure composition of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G samples (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>c). Two distinct peaks at ∼1320 and ∼1590 cm<sup>–1</sup> correspond to the D band and the G band, verifying the existence of disordered graphite carbon and sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized carbon atoms, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref25" aria-label="reference 25">(25)</a> These results correspond well with XRD analysis, indicating the successful synthesis of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites. XPS was carried out to detect the elemental composition and chemical valence of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites. The survey spectra of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G primarily consist of Sb, C, O, and V elements (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>d). For the high-resolution V 2p spectrum of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G, two primitive peaks of V 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and V 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals are exhibited in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>e. These two asymmetric peaks can be further divided into V<sup>5+</sup> (516.9 and 524.2 eV) and V<sup>4+</sup> (515.8 and 523.1 eV), indicating the mixed-valence state of V in the materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref26" aria-label="reference 26">(26)</a> The Sb 3d located at 531.18 and 540.58 eV (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">2</a>f) correspond to the Sb 3d<sub>5/2</sub> and Sb 3d<sub>3/2</sub> orbitals, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a></div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/medium/am0c21676_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0002.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Schematic illustration for the preparation process of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c21676&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/medium/am0c21676_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0003.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. (a) XRD patterns of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites and pure SbVO<sub>4</sub>. (b) Crystal structure of tetragonal SbVO<sub>4</sub>. (c) Raman spectra of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites. The XPS spectrum of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites: (d) XPS survey spectrum, (e) high-resolution V 2p XPS spectra, and (f) high-resolution Sb 3d XPS spectra.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c21676&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The morphology and microstructures of the materials were revealed by SEM characterization. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>a,b, pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> is mainly in the form of nanoparticles, showing obvious particle agglomeration. By contract, the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites exhibit that evenly distributed SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles are embedded on the surface of graphene (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>c,d). The average particle size of SbVO<sub>4</sub> in SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites is about 30 nm, which is smaller than that of the bare SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles (the average size is 70 nm). The small particle size of SbVO<sub>4</sub> embedded in the graphene substrate can increase the intimate contact with the electrolyte, increase the number of active sites, and shorten the ion transport pathway, which could be favorable to the improvement of cycling and rate performance for lithium and sodium storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> The EDS mapping of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>e–i; Sb, V, O, and C elements are well distributed within the structure of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G, indicating that the SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles are uniformly embedded in the graphene substrate. The specific surface area and pore structure of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G and SbVO<sub>4</sub> were obtained by the N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption measurement. The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) specific surface area and the average pore size of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G are 130 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 5 nm, respectively. (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>j and<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a>). In contrast, the BET specific surface area and the pore size of SbVO<sub>4</sub> are calculated to be 93 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>–1</sup> and 9 nm, respectively. The combination of the SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles and graphene was also explored by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) characterization. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>k, the SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles are well embedded in the graphene substrate. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">3</a>l) displays a lattice distance of 0.41 nm, which indexes to the (111) plane of SbVO<sub>4</sub>.</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/medium/am0c21676_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0004.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. SEM images of (a, b) pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> and (c, d) SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites. The inset of (b) and (d): particles size of (b) SbVO<sub>4</sub> and (d) SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G, respectively. (e) SEM images of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G. (f, g) EDS elemental mapping of Sb, V, O, and C of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G.(j) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption–desorption isotherms for SbVO<sub>4</sub> and SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G. (k) TEM image and (l) HRTEM image of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c21676&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The electrochemical performance of SbVO<sub>4</sub> and SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G for LIB anodes was measured in half cells. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>a shows the CV curves of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrode at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The results of the CV profiles are consistent with the previously reported results. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> The electrochemical reactions are revealed by the following reactions <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/medium/am0c21676_m001.gif" alt="" id="_i8"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></span><span class="NLM_disp-formula"><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><img class="imgWithoutFigViewer " src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/medium/am0c21676_m002.gif" alt="" id="_i9"></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></span></div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/medium/am0c21676_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0005.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Electrochemical properties of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G and SbVO<sub>4</sub> as anodes for LIBs. (a) CV curves of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) GCD profiles of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycling performance at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate performances at different current densities. (e) Nyquist plots of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G and pure SbVO<sub>4</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c21676&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Three obvious reduction peaks of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrodes at 1.25, 0.61, and 0.35–0.01 V can be seen in the first anodic scanning, which mainly correspond to the generation of Sb, Li<sub>3</sub>Sb (Li<sub>2</sub>Sb), and solid electrolyte interface (SEI) films. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref23 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref23 ref29" aria-label="reference 22, 23 and 29">(22,23,29)</a> The reduction peak at 1.25 V in the second cycle changes significantly, indicating that <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq </a><a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="2">2</a> is an incomplete reversible process. However, the CV curves almost overlap in the following cycles, revealing the distinguished reversibility and stability of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites. The GCD curves of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G and pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles under different cycles were evaluated to further investigate the electrochemical reactions during the Li<sup>+</sup> insertion/deinsertion processes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S2</a>). The discharge platforms at 1.3 and 0.7 V represent the generation of Sb and Li<sub>3</sub>Sb (Li<sub>2</sub>Sb). A small platform at 0.2 V can be observed, which corresponds to the formation of SEI films. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22 ref30'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22 ref30" aria-label="reference 22 and 30">(22,30)</a> In the subsequent cycles, the discharge platforms from the 10th to 100th cycle remain nearly unchanged, indicating excellent electrochemical reversibility and stability of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites. The results of the charge/discharge curves of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>b) well coincide with the CV analysis, further demonstrating the multistep electrochemical lithiation/delithiation processes of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites. In contrast, the pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles displayed a significantly irreversible reaction during the Li<sup>+</sup> insertion/deinsertion processes. The discharge platform of SbVO<sub>4</sub> at 0.7 V gradually becomes shorter (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>), directly resulting in the rapid attenuation of capacity during the cycling process. The SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites display high initial discharge and charge capacities of 1394 and 998.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G shows a higher initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 71.64% when compared with that of the SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles (ICE of 65.33%). The low ICE is mainly derived from the electrolyte decomposition and SEI film formation. The Coulombic efficiency (CE) of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G increases to 98% from the fifth cycle. Surprisingly, the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrode shows a certain capacity increase, which might be arising from the activation process of the electrode materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref31" aria-label="reference 31">(31)</a> The SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composite electrode remains 1079.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>c). Nevertheless, the capacity of SbVO<sub>4</sub> gradually decreases and drops to 583.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> (only 69.5% of the initial capacity) after 150 cycles. The poor structural stability and rapid capacity decline of SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles can be ascribed to the material pulverization and agglomeration as a result of the huge volume changes in the alloying process.</div><div class="NLM_p">The rate performances of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites and SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles for LIB anodes are conducted at 0.1–3.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, as presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>d and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S3a</a>. The SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites display reversible capacities of 1070.1, 1025.4, 962.75, 917.95, 864.9, and 813.85 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 3.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In contract, the SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles exhibit poor capacities of 867.95, 817.3, 667.2, 546.05, 430.2, and 344.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 3.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. As the current density comes back to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, an excellent reversible capacity of 1047.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is obtained, revealing the well-preserved nanostructure and good reversibility of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites. The charge/discharge curves of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites and pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> electrodes at different current densities are displayed in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3b,c</a>. The discharge platform near 0.7 V is basically stable, suggesting the excellent stability of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G anode. In sharp contrast, the charge/discharge curves of SbVO<sub>4</sub> change greatly, where the rapid shrinkage platform at 0.7 V leads to a low capacity of SbVO<sub>4</sub>. In addition, the discharge platform of SbVO<sub>4</sub> gradually moves to a high potential resulting in serious polarization. The EIS of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G and SbVO<sub>4</sub> electrodes are performed to investigate the electrochemical kinetics for lithium and sodium storage. In EIS spectra, the high-frequency depressed semicircle reflects the impedance of the interface membrane (<i>R</i><sub>S</sub>) and contact resistance (<i>R</i><sub>f</sub>), and the middle represents interfacial charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>), while the slope is related to the Warburg diffusion (<i>Z</i><sub>W</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a> The SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G anode displays a smaller <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value compared to SbVO<sub>4</sub> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">4</a>e), indicating that the composite structure of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G improves the conductivity.</div><div class="NLM_p">The SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites and pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> are further applied as anodes for SIBs. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S4</a> show the CV curves of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G and pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> electrodes with 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. The characteristics of the CV curves are very similar to those of Sb<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5 ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref5 ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 5, 33 and 34">(5,33,34)</a> The wide reduction peak at 0.6–0.01 V can be observed during the first anodic scanning (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>a), corresponding to the SEI film and Sb forming. The reduction and oxidation peaks of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G at 0.83 and 1.78 V can be attributed to the alloying reaction between Sb and Na. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> The GCD curves of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites and SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles were measured during different cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites and SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles exhibit initial discharge capacities of 744.9 and 680.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> with ICE of 68.93 and 36.98%, respectively (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>b and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S5</a>). The lower ICE for SbVO<sub>4</sub> is mainly attributed to the decomposition of the electrolyte and the irreversible redox reaction. A quite apparent long discharge platform at 0.5 V is shown in the first cycle for both SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G and SbVO<sub>4</sub>, which corresponds to the formation of the SEI layer and Na<sub>3</sub>Sb. After the 30th cycle, the charge/discharge platforms of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composite anodes remained almost unchanged, which may be caused by the gradual stable optimization of the SEI film and its robust structure. In contrast, the charge/discharge platforms of SbVO<sub>4</sub> disappear after 100 cycles, which could have been originated from the structure pulverization of SbVO<sub>4</sub> (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>c, the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G anode still maintains a discharge specific capacity of 401.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 450 cycles. By comparison, the pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> anode shows a considerable capacity fade (less than 100 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 60 cycles). In addition, the reversible capacities of 525.16, 408.9, 364, 321, and 291 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 3.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> are obtained for the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>d,e), which are significantly higher than those of pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles. As the current returns to a low current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites still have a capacity of 467.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, indicating preeminent reversibility. The high-rate cycling performance of the two samples was evaluated at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">5</a>f). The SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrode delivers a discharge specific capacity of 283.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 380 cycles at a current density of 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>. By sharp comparison, the SbVO<sub>4</sub> anode suffers from inferior stability and the capacity rapidly declines to 60 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 30 cycles, indicating that the structure of pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles is severely damaged during the cycle. EIS was tested to investigate the electrochemical kinetics of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites and pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> anodes for SIBs. The SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrode exhibits a lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value than the pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>), suggesting that the hierarchical structure of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites can offer more accessible active areas for charge transfer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a></div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/medium/am0c21676_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0006.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Electrochemical properties of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G and SbVO<sub>4</sub> as anodes for SIBs. (a) CV curves of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. (b) GCD curves of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) Cycling performance at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. (d) Rate performances. (e) Average discharge specific capacity at different current densities. (f) Cycling performances at 1.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites and pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c21676&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">The phase evolutions of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G at different discharge/charge states were revealed by ex situ XRD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>a,b). Almost no obvious diffraction peaks were detected upon lithiation/delithiation, which may be due to the amorphous structure of Li<sub>2</sub>O wrapping the Li<sub>3</sub>Sb and V nanoparticles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> No peaks of Sb were detected in the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrode surface during the discharging/charging process (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>c). This result may indicate that the Li<sub>2</sub>O and SEI films were formed in the outermost of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23" aria-label="reference 23">(23)</a> The possible reaction mechanism was further checked by HRTEM (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d,e). At the full discharge stage, the amorphous area and lattice fringe of Li<sub>3</sub>Sb were observed in the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrode (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>d). These results confirmed that SbVO<sub>4</sub> transformed into Li<sub>3</sub>Sb and Li<sub>2</sub>O. During the delithiation process, SbVO<sub>4</sub> can be reformed after being fully charged (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>e). The formation of the amorphous structure may result in the capacity increase of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G in LIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref38" aria-label="reference 38">(38)</a> The morphology of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G after 50 cycles in LIBs and SIBs was observed by SEM (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S7a,b</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>f,g). The results showed that no cracking occurred both in LIBs and SIBs (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7a,b</a>). The initial morphology of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites is still maintained after 50 cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">6</a>f,g). These results indicate that the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composite electrode material possesses excellent structure stability.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/medium/am0c21676_0007.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0007.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) XRD patterns of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G in different discharge/charge states, and corresponding (b) discharge/charge voltage profiles of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G for the first cycle at 100 mA g<sup>–1</sup>. (c) XPS spectra of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrode surface in LIBs. HRTEM images of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G in (d) fully discharged and (e) fully charged in LIBs. (f, g) SEM images of the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrode for LIBs and SIBs after 50 cycles.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0007.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c21676&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Compared to the lithium storage of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G, SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G possesses larger volume changes and more serious structural damages during the SbVO<sub>4</sub> sodiation/desodiation reaction resulting from the slow electrochemical reaction kinetics and the unstable SEI layer caused by the larger ion radius of the Na ion compared to that of the Li ion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39" aria-label="reference 39">(39)</a> Therefore, SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G displayed a more obvious capacity fade during the first several cycles in SIBs than in LIBs. However, the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G anode exhibited superior long cycle life in SIBs than in LIBs. It is generally believed that the formed Li<sub>3</sub>Sb possesses a rock salt structure, which is different from Na<sub>3</sub>Sb with a hexagonal phase. The slight volume expansion in the hexagonal Na<sub>3</sub>Sb phase contributes to the stable cycling performance for sodium storage. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40" aria-label="reference 40">(40)</a> The cycling performance of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G anodes is better than those of the lately reported LIB and SIB anodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>a,b). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref41 ref42 ref43 ref44 ref45 ref46 ref47 ref48 ref49 ref50 ref51 ref52" aria-label="reference 23 and 41 to 52">(23,41−52)</a> The poor electrochemical properties of the pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> electrodes stem from the agglomeration of nanoparticles, which results in insufficient space to relieve volume expansion, thus the structure of the SbVO<sub>4</sub> material collapses during repeated cycles (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>c). Fortunately, in SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composite electrodes, SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles acting as an active material provide a high electrochemical activity, meanwhile, graphene contributes to the reversible transport of electrons/ions and maintains structural stability. Second, graphene effectively inhibits the self-agglomeration of SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles, thus promoting SbVO<sub>4</sub> particles to fully store Li<sup>+</sup> or Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Third, the small SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles shorten the ion diffusion path, improving the reaction kinetics (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure </a><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">7</a>d). Therefore, the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composite anodes exhibit more outstanding cycle performance and rate property than pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> nanoparticles.</div><figure id="fig7" data-index="7" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 7</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/medium/am0c21676_0008.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0008.jpeg" id="gr7" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 7. Cycling performance comparison with reported (a) LIB anodes and (b) SIB anodes (rGO: reduced graphene oxide and CNT: carbon nanotube). Structural illustration of (c) SbVO<sub>4</sub> and (d) SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrodes during Li<sup>+</sup>/Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/deinsertion.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/asset/images/large/am0c21676_0008.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.0c21676&amp;id=fig7"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i10">Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites have been synthesized by hydrothermal and freeze-drying methods. The synergistic effect between SbVO<sub>4</sub> and graphene with alloying and conversion reaction, structural integrity, and enhanced conductivity contributes to high capacity, cycling stability, and high rate performance. When used as anodes in LIBs and SIBs, the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrodes exhibit a high capacity of 1079.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 150 cycles in LIBs and 401.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> after 450 cycles in SIBs. In addition, SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G achieves impressive rate performances of 813.85 and 291 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 3.0 A g<sup>–1</sup> for LIBs and SIBs, respectively. The outstanding electrochemical performance is mainly attributed to the unique structure and synergistic effect of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites. This work demonstrates that the SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composites are promising anodes for both LIBs and SIBs.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes-1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c21676?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.0c21676</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Pore size of pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> and SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G; GCD profiles of pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>; average discharge specific capacity of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G and pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> as LIB anodes at different current densities; GCD curves of pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> and SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G composite electrodes at current densities of 0.1–3.0 A g<sup>–1</sup>; CV curves of pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> electrodes in SIBs at 0.2 mV s<sup>–1</sup>; GCD profiles of pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> electrodes in SIBs at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>; Nyquist plots of SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G and pure SbVO<sub>4</sub> in SIBs; and SEM images of the fully discharged SbVO<sub>4</sub>/G electrode for LIBs and SIBs after 50 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.0c21676/suppl_file/am0c21676_si_001.pdf">am0c21676_si_001.pdf (403.04 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.0c21676" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit4R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">4</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical insertion of sodium into carbon</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Doeff, Marca M.; Ma, Yanping; Visco, Steven J.; De Jonghe, Lutgard C.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">140</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Electrochem. insertion of sodium ions into carbon using solid polymer electrolytes or org. liq. electrolytes is described.  Cells with the configuration Na/P(EO)6NaCF3SO3/CP(EO) (PEO = polyethylene oxide) or Na/liq. electrolyte/C were galvanostatically discharged, charged, and cycled.  The extent of insertion into C (i.e., x in Na+Cax-) was found to be a strong function of the type and particle size of the carbon used, and the reversibility of the process was highly dependent upon the type of electrolyte used.  The possibility of design a sodium ion rocking chair cell is discussed, and a first-generation example, using a petroleum coke anode, polymer electrolyte, and sodium cobalt bronze cathode is described.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGocbintpirKnrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSzYqh_c3GFQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADyaK2cXhsVKqu7o%253D&amp;md5=a50a9d5ed6dd7cc569094cf9ab06beb4</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201600943"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, M.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Sodium-ion batteries: present and future</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hwang, Jang-Yeon; Myung, Seung-Taek; Sun, Yang-Kook</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Society Reviews</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2017</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">46</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">3529-3614</span>CODEN:

        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGot-2IKXj-Q-7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSzYqh_c3GFQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXkvF2rsbk%253D&amp;md5=cfc7b2d153696dcbded22d954ff06174</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/aenm.201800930"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shi, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Fang, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cheng, H.                         cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Update on anode materials for Na-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kang, Hongyan; Liu, Yongchang; Cao, Kangzhe; Zhao, Yan; Jiao, Lifang; Wang, Yijing; Yuan, Huatang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Materials Chemistry A: Materials for Energy and Sustainability</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion batteries have emerged in recent years, due to their advantages of natural abundance, low cost and environmental friendliness.  In this article, we review the up-to-date research progress on anode materials for Na-ion batteries from five respects: carbon-based materials, alloy-based materials, metal oxides and sulfides based on conversion reaction, titanium-based compds. with insertion mechanism, and org. composites.  In particular, we not only summarize the Na-storage mechanism of these anodes, but also discuss the failure mechanism.  The problems and challenges assocd. with these anodes are pointed out.  Furthermore, on the basis of extensive reports and our exptl. studies, feasible strategies are suggested for designing high performance anode materials.  After further in-depth exploration and investigation, we believe that Na-ion batteries are promising alternative to lithium-ion batteries for low cost and large-scale energy storage systems in the near future.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrB4Pf2vZ5QrrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhSzYqh_c3GFQ"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXhtFelsrnN&amp;md5=8311789a2dc4e61dabff177245235c3e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1021/jz2012066"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Slater, M.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Amorphous TiO2 Nanotube Anode for Rechargeable Sodium Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xiong, Hui; Slater, Michael D.; Balasubramanian, Mahalingam; Johnson, Christopher S.; Rajh, Tijana</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physical Chemistry Letters</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries are an attractive alternative to lithium-ion batteries that alleviate problems with lithium availability and cost.  Despite several studies of cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries involving layered oxide materials, there are few low-voltage metal oxide anodes capable of operating sodium ion reversibly at room temp.  We have synthesized amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube electrodes directly grown on current collectors without binders and additives to use as an anode for sodium-ion batteries.  We find that only amorphous large diam. nanotubes (&gt;80 nm I.D.) can support electrochem. cycling with sodium ions.  These electrodes maximize their capacity in operando and reach reversible capacity of 150 mA-h/g in 15 cycles.  We also demonstrate for the first time a full cell all-oxide sodium-ion battery using amorphous titanium dioxide nanotube anode coupled to a Na1.0Li0.2Ni0.25Mn0.75Oδ cathode at room temp. exhibiting good rate capability.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr8q2bDG7YJCbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liw9qDvuzID1w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3MXht1ChtLbP&amp;md5=66a2a71991a01d6823c9c01024f65c74</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1021/nl402747x"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Kim, K.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Anatase Titania Nanorods as an Intercalation Anode Material for Rechargeable Sodium Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Kim, Ki-Tae; Ali, Ghulam; Chung, Kyung Yoon; Yoon, Chong Seung; Yashiro, Hitoshi; Sun, Yang-Kook; Lu, Jun; Amine, Khalil; Myung, Seung-Taek</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">14</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">416-422</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">We report the electrochem. activity of anatase TiO2 nanorods in a Na cell.  The anatase TiO2 nanorods were synthesized by a hydrothermal method, and their surfaces were coated by carbon to improve the elec. cond. by carbonization of pitch at 700° for 2 h in Ar flow.  The resulting structure does not change before and after the carbon coating, as confirmed by x-ray diffraction.  Transmission electron microscopy images confirm the presence of a carbon coating on the anatase TiO2 nanorods.  In cell tests, anodes of bare and carbon-coated anatase TiO2 nanorods exhibit stable cycling performance and attain a capacity of about 172 and 193 mA-h/g on the first charge, resp., in the voltage range of 0-3 V.  With the help of the conductive carbon layers, the carbon-coated anatase TiO2 delivers more capacity at high rates, 104 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g), 82 mA-h/g at the 30C rate (10 A/g), and 53 mA-h/g at the 100C rate (33 A g-1).  By contrast, the anode of bare anatase TiO2 nanorods delivers only about 38 mA-h/g at the 10C rate (3.3 A/g).  The excellent cyclability and high-rate capability are the result of a Na+ insertion and extn. reaction into the host structure coupled with Ti4+/3+ redox reaction, as shown by x-ray absorption spectroscopy.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpadjcIpWM-W7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0liw9qDvuzID1w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2cXktlGhsA%253D%253D&amp;md5=4bc3a520e6f654a82cbee3b778eef0e7</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref11"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">11</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit11" data-doi="10.1002/adfm.201904398"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiong, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mai, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Niu, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">An, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Vanadium-Based Nanomaterials: A Promising Family for Emerging Metal-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>Adv. Funct Mater.</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2020</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>30</i></span>, <span class="NLM_elocation-id">1904398</span> <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:
                10.1002/adfm.201904398</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D30%26publication_year%3D2020%26journal%3DAdv.%2BFunct%2BMater.%26author%3DX.%2BXu%26author%3DF.%2BXiong%26author%3DJ.%2BMeng%26author%3DX.%2BWang%26author%3DL.%2BMai%26author%3DC.%2BNiu%26author%3DQ.%2BAn%26title%3DVanadium-Based%2BNanomaterials%253A%2BA%2BPromising%2BFamily%2Bfor%2BEmerging%2BMetal-Ion%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1002%252Fadfm.201904398&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.0c21676&amp;doiOfLink=10.1002%2Fadfm.201904398&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Vanadium-Based Nanomaterials: A Promising Family for Emerging Metal-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Xu, Xiaoming; Xiong, Fangyu; Meng, Jiashen; Wang, Xuanpeng; Niu, Chaojiang; An, Qinyou; Mai, Liqiang</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Functional Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">30</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">10</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">1904398</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AFMDC6</span>;
